I said: I'm supposed to ask you that. And there's no right to remain silent or anything like that? No, no, no. There is no such thing. There is torture and the people were crawling. Later I found out that one kid, 17 years old, he died in the same area I was. After a few weeks, Ali and his family were released. His dad was able to pull some strings to get them out. Ali left Saudi Arabia for good in the 1990s, relocating to the United States. Around that time, a young MBS was busy playing video games and surfing the newly minted worldwide web. No plans for the throne were on the horizon. His father was still number three on the list. There were two other older brothers before him. And even if his father somehow ended up as king, MBS wasn't the logical successor. His mom was the third wife and he had several older half-brothers. It was kind of unimaginable. Plus, the royal family had ballooned. Ibn Saud's many kids had many kids. We're talking thousands. Different branches of the family controlled different parts of the government, and it was easy to get lost in the shuffle. MBS knew he had to find a way to stand out. So unlike his older half-brothers who spent a lot of time abroad—they almost sound like posh British people—MBS decided to stay in Saudi Arabia. He went to university in Saudi Arabia. His English wasn't even that great for a long time. He wanted to be close to his father, Salman. He was kind of the family disciplinarian, handling these different family disputes. So he had files on everybody. All that intel his father had went into a kind of burn book, which MBS had access to and which he would later use to consolidate power. And then in less than a year, MBS's fortunes changed dramatically. Between October 2011 and June 2012, two of MBS's uncles died. They were the older brothers in line for the throne before his father. With their deaths, his father became essentially the guaranteed second in line to be the king. Suddenly MBS had a clear path to power. But there were others in the royal family vying for that spot, and MBS knew he had to move fast. It was like almost Shakespearean, people conniving against Salman coming into power. MBS put together a team of people he trusted and they were using everything they could, even what you might consider espionage techniques to understand what everyone else was doing. MBS just became a round-the-clock tactician, clearing the path for his father to take the throne and proving himself indispensable. He switched gears from the young kind of business-oriented guy into this kind of king in waiting. In early 2015, word began to spread that the sitting King of Saudi Arabia, King Abdullah, was very sick. MBS actually went to King Abdullah with his father in the hospital and King Abdullah was on a respirator; he was basically dead. Something those closest to him were trying to conceal for as long as possible. And MBS barged into the room. The secretary of the King was there to make sure nobody sees the king on a respirator. MBS slapped him across the face. That slap led to the announcement of King Abdullah being dead. Salman himself was getting up there in age. So when he took the throne, MBS would be pulling the strings. His cousin told me he is like a bull in a china shop. What he says goes no matter what anybody else says. "Send in the F-15s." That's the order MBS gave to a room of stunned Saudi generals just eight weeks after his father Salman took the throne. Almost overnight, MBS had consolidated control over the kingdom's finances and military. He installed a close ally as head of Aramco and he launched a deadly bombing campaign in Yemen. These were American-made bombs the Obama administration sold him. MBS hoped he could quickly stomp out the Houthi rebels there who were backed by Saudi Arabia's longtime rival, Iran. He's saying this will be over in a couple of months. Then it becomes a, you know, decades-long problem and nobody knows that better than America. The way things get done in the world are through a combination of focus and personal connection. The third factor is self-belief. His plan involved converting the PIF into a Silicon Valley super engine. Witkoff and Kushner were also in charge of the failed nuclear negotiations that preceded the war with Iran. When the war began, retaliatory strikes on Saudi Arabia and other Gulf countries followed. In late March, there were reportedly a number of calls between MBS and Trump in which MBS urged him to continue the war, seeing it as, quote: a historic opportunity to remake the region. Iran is Saudi Arabia's longtime rival, and Saudi Arabia has historically marketed itself as a key defender of Palestinians. In private, MBS has reportedly said he doesn't care personally about the Palestinian issue, though a Saudi official later disputed this. He's also indicated he may eventually move to recognize Israel if there's a clear path to a Palestinian state and if the price is right. The custodian of the two holy cities of Mecca and Medina agreeing to recognize Israel in that way is such a big thing. He's also a deal guy, just like Witkoff and Kushner, and he's thinking: If I'm going to do that deal, I'm going to make sure that I really get something that's really strong. And what he wants is things like a security relationship that's maybe similar or even better than the Qatar one. He also wants to have nuclear power in Saudi Arabia. MBS is already doing some business discreetly with Israel, especially in the cybersecurity and surveillance sector. And now there's a new superpower that it seems like everyone is racing towards: AI. For us, like the average people, a lot of us are interacting with AI in terms of chatbots and sort of a tool for work and things like that. But on a higher level, a governmental level, AI and all of that is also being conceived as surveillance technology, defense tech, and military tech. Yeah, I think Silicon Valley is helping build up the global surveillance state, and I think that's a good term for it. Consider what Larry Ellison, the founder of Oracle, said: The police will be on their best behavior because we were... we were constantly recording, watching, and recording everything that's going on. Citizens will be on their best behavior because we're constantly recording and reporting everything that's going on. I think what was happening before was perhaps Silicon Valley didn't realize that they were creating tools of control, that something like Twitter could become a tool of control. I think now Silicon Valley knows that they are making tools of control. And, you know, how is AI being used now? Well, it's being used in Gaza to pick out targets for the IDF to bomb people. You know, us here in America, for the border patrol and ICE now to find people to deport. It's these much more draconian, aggressive, top-down security state style forms of governance that we're seeing AI enable. The greater Silicon Valley is supported by dictatorships who want these surveillance tools. Ali al-Ahmed has been in the US for 30 years now. He's continued to be a vocal critic of the Saudi regime, even though that's meant he can no longer be in contact with his family back in Saudi Arabia to protect them from repercussions. I want to be with my parents and with my family. I love my country. I love my land. He's received threats from the Saudi government and also offers. I got offers of millions of dollars. This is because they do not fathom anyone outside and inside to speak freely. But Ali is not a deal guy. I can sleep on the floor. I can eat a piece of bread and a slice of tomato and I'll be fine. I will not complain. The thing I want is to be free. To talk, to say something. That freedom of me speaking my mind, I cannot put a number on it. And that's it for this week's show. LGT’s Prince Max Urges Global Asset Diversification

Bloomberg Live(184.0K) ·24:56 ·195 views · 2026-03-26
Prince Max von und zu Liechtenstein (Chairman, LGT) David Ingles (Anchor, Bloomberg)
To navigate escalating geopolitical instability and the rapid onset of AI, Prince Max urges investors to prioritize global geographic diversification and integrate long-term "purpose" into family governance to ensure multi-generational survival.
TL;DR To navigate escalating geopolitical instability and the rapid onset of AI, Prince Max urges investors to prioritize global geographic diversification and integrate long-term "purpose" into family governance to ensure multi-generational survival. Rising Nationalism: Prince...

TL;DR

To navigate escalating geopolitical instability and the rapid onset of AI, Prince Max urges investors to prioritize global geographic diversification and integrate long-term "purpose" into family governance to ensure multi-generational survival.

Geopolitical Risks and the Global Outlook

  • Rising Nationalism: Prince Max expressed deep concern regarding the shift toward "excessive and stupid nationalism," noting that current geopolitical trends "rhyme" with the late 19th and early 20th centuries which led to global conflict.
      • Shift from Multilateralism: He warned that the world is moving away from the post-WWII mindset of collaboration and multilateral agreements that historically grew the "global pie."
  • Artificial Intelligence: Identified as a primary "enabling technology" that will fundamentally disrupt businesses, governments, and society.
      • Investor Challenge: The speed of AI development requires investors to remain highly agile and "nervous" enough to respond to rapid structural changes.
  • Historical Precedent: Drawing from the Princely House of Liechtenstein’s history, he noted the family lost the majority of its assets twice due to political disruption, surviving only through broad diversification.

Investment Strategy and Portfolio Construction

  • Target Returns: A "decent" annual yield for long-term investors is between 5% and 10%, depending on specific risk profiles and liquidity requirements.
  • Asset Allocation:
      • Equity Bias: Long-term investors without immediate liquidity needs should be invested "predominantly in equities."
      • Dry Powder: Maintaining a cash reserve is essential to capitalize on "rainy day" opportunities when markets downturn.
  • Public vs. Private Markets: Prince Max argued that the fundamental volatility of high-quality public companies is similar to their private counterparts; the primary difference is daily trading transparency rather than underlying risk.
  • Geographic Diversification: Increased political risk makes spreading assets across different jurisdictions more critical than in previous decades.

Generational Stewardship and Governance

  • The "Purpose" Anchor: Long-lived institutions (universities, religious organizations, and states) survive because they have a defined purpose. Wealthy families must integrate purpose into their constitutions to remain relevant.
  • Formal Governance: He strongly recommends "documenting intent" through family constitutions.
      • Liechtenstein Model: He cited his family’s 300-year-old "constitution" and their acquisition of governance rights (the formation of the state of Liechtenstein via "M&A") as key drivers of identity and motivation.
  • Multi-Generational Strategy: Success requires a focus on quality people and the cultivation of deep, reliable networks to navigate periods of high "noise."

Reframing Impact and Performance Metrics

  • Rejecting Trade-offs: Prince Max argued against the idea of a trade-off between profit and social impact. He advocates for a holistic view where "negative externalities" are weighed against financial returns.
  • Modernizing Metrics: He noted that current financial and economic metrics were designed for a different era.
      • Holistic Value Add: Investors should move toward a "blank sheet" approach that accounts for modern societal challenges and long-term value creation beyond simple P&L.

***

Notable Quotes

"Twice in our history we have lost most of our assets due to significant political changes and disruptions. Gladly, we were diversified enough to, even after having lost quite a bit, to retain enough to recover." — Prince Max von und zu Liechtenstein
"If you don't need liquidity, you should be invested predominantly in equities... you should seek decent equity returns. That’s what I think a truly long-term investor... should seek." — Prince Max von und zu Liechtenstein
"History once more doesn't quite repeat itself, but it sort of rhymes... People don't seem to remember what created these horrible world wars and it had a lot to do with excessive and stupid nationalism. I feel that I see too much of that when I’m analyzing the current geopolitics." — Prince Max von und zu Liechtenstein
I was thinking for a way to start, but my thought process got interrupted by checking my phone, just looking at the latest headlines. That's really how gripped we are with very sudden—a lot is happening. In the news business we're extremely fatigued given all the developments, but such is the world that we live in. Useful statistics, if useful at all: the last six years, at least three wars, one pandemic, 100-plus record highs in the S&P, and a $50 or $60 swing in oil. It even went negative when you look at West Texas. And it's important to—I think the reason I bring this up is—how do you, and I know you hear this a lot, how do you differentiate signal from noise? If you wait long enough, some noise becomes signal. And how do you invest through the noise, and what to sort of tune out? LIVE: European Parliament Votes on US Trade Deal | Trump Tariff News | Trade Deal | US-EU Trade Deal

Firstpost(9.5M) ·68:56 ·2.8K views · 2026-03-26
Donald Trump (President of the United States)
The European Parliament is debating a mandate for the "Turnberry" trade deal with the United States, aiming to secure economic stability while implementing "Trump-proof" safeguards—such as suspension and sunset clauses—to counter imbalanced tariffs and unilateral US policy shifts.
TL;DR The European Parliament is debating a mandate for the "Turnberry" trade deal with the United States, aiming to secure economic stability while implementing "Trump-proof" safeguards—such as suspension and sunset clauses—to counter imbalanced tariffs and unilateral US policy...

TL;DR

The European Parliament is debating a mandate for the "Turnberry" trade deal with the United States, aiming to secure economic stability while implementing "Trump-proof" safeguards—such as suspension and sunset clauses—to counter imbalanced tariffs and unilateral US policy shifts.

The Economic Necessity of the Transatlantic Partnership

  • Scale of Trade: The US remains the EU's primary trading partner and export destination, with €1.7 trillion in goods and services traded in 2024–2025 and €4.8 trillion in mutual investment.
      • Employment Impact: Approximately 16 million jobs depend on the transatlantic economy.
      • Cost of Inaction: Željana Zovko (EPP) noted that every year of delay in trade normalization costs the European economy roughly €5 billion.
  • Predictability vs. Volatility: Commissioner Valdis Dombrovskis emphasized that the deal is an "indispensable step" to provide stability for European businesses and workers amidst global upheaval.
      • Danuše Nerudová (Budget Committee) cautioned that while the deal is worth the access, it currently "costs us four billion euros per year," a sum equivalent to the Erasmus program.

Structural Imbalances and the "Turnberry" Agreement

  • Tariff Disparity: Multiple speakers, including Rapporteur Bernd Lange, criticized the current "one-sided" nature of the deal.
      • The 15% Gap: The US currently imposes an average of 15% duties on European goods (up from a historical 3%), while the EU is expected to maintain zero tariffs on US industrial and agricultural imports.
      • Steel and Aluminum: Serious concerns remain regarding the 50% duties on steel and aluminum derivatives, which were increased by the US even after the deal was initially reached.
  • Sectoral Concerns:
      • Agriculture: Veronika Vrecionová (AGRI) stressed that European farmers need "fair conditions" and predictability to compete in the US market.
      • Energy Independence: Members of the Left and Greens, including Martin Schirdewan and Anna Cavazzini, criticized commitments to import US LNG, arguing it trades dependence on Russia for dependence on the US.

"Trump-Proofing" Through Legislative Safeguards

  • The "6 S" Framework: Rapporteur Bernd Lange proposed a strategy to turn a "bad deal" into a "good agreement" through six specific requirements:

1. Sunrise Clause: Setting clear conditions for the deal to begin.

2. Suspension Clause: Allowing the EU to immediately reinstate tariffs if the US violates commitments.

3. Sunset Clause: Mandatory reassessment of the deal after a set period.

4. Safeguards: Protection for specific industrial sectors.

5. Steel Clarity: Ensuring strict definitions to prevent "derivative" product tariff hikes.

6. Strong Parliament Involvement: Ensuring the legislature oversees all ongoing negotiations.

  • Political Leverage: Kathleen Van Brempt (S&D) stated the group would not offer a "blank check" or "free pass," insisting that final approval depends on the US honoring commitments to lower steel tariffs.

Geopolitical Strategy and "Global Gateway"

  • Strategic Autonomy: While some members (e.g., Hermann Tertsch) warned against "anti-American fobia," others argued the EU must use its Anti-Coercion Instrument to resist "bullying" and "blackmail" regarding Greenland or digital legislation.
  • Global Gateway Initiative: The Parliament also reviewed the €306 billion Global Gateway project, intended as a "virtuous alternative" to China’s Belt and Road Initiative.
      • Transparency Needs: Chloe Ridolfi described the initiative as currently "too opaque" and called for better tracking of local value-added benefits and human rights safeguards.
      • Private Sector Role: Hildegard Bentele emphasized the need for an "Investment Hub" to act as a one-stop-shop for European businesses to participate in global infrastructure projects.

***

Notable Quotes

"It’s clear to me that a deal is not an agreement. An agreement that we negotiate has commitments and safeguards and a dispute settlement mechanism... all of that is not in the deal."

— Bernd Lange, MEP and Rapporteur

"A deal is a deal and our credibility depends on keeping our word... we will not turn a blind eye to any risks to our interests."

— Valdis Dombrovskis, European Commissioner for Trade

"We will not be intimidated. We will not be bullied into a bad deal... this European Parliament stands up for [citizen] interests and for our Europe."

— Kathleen Van Brempt, MEP (S&D Group)

"The current occupant of the White House changes minds almost every day... this is why we need a clear clause. We implement this deal only if the United States respects it."

— Danuše Nerudová, MEP (Budget Committee)

Ďakujem veľmi pekne. A teraz poprosím za komisiu pána komisára Dombrovskisa, nech sa páči. Mr. Chair, honorable members. Today's debate and the vote that will follow mark a very important milestone in our efforts to provide stability and predictability to European business, workers, and citizens in our trade relationships with the United States. I'm here today to replace Commissioner Šefčovič, who could not be present as he is in Cameroon leading the EU delegation at the WTO ministerial conference MC14. The Turnberry agreement negotiated with the United States in July last year and the EU-US joint statement of 21st August steadied our trade relationship at a time of profound upheaval. After the US overhaul of its trade policy, the deal provided predictability for European companies and consumers when they needed it most. We recognize many of the concerns members of this house have raised, particularly with regards to tariffs on steel and aluminum and their derivative products. We also heard your calls for reassurances about the US commitment to uphold the deal and the need for safeguards should the terms be violated. Yet despite these valid concerns, one truth remains valid: our economic interests demand that we move forward. We must act in the best interest of our firms, our workers, and our citizens, especially under the current circumstances. Honoring our commitments under the joint statement does precisely that. A deal is a deal and our credibility depends on keeping our word. And let's not forget the scale of what is at stake. The US remains our number one trading partner and our number one export destination by far. Our supply chains and business ties have been forged over decades and underpin our industrial base today and sustain millions of European jobs. In 2024-25 alone, 1.7 trillion euros of goods and services were traded across the Atlantic. We have invested in each other's economies 4.8 trillion euros. All in all, our trade with the US accounts for around 20% of our global trade. Let me tell you why today's vote matters so much. The implementation of our commitments under the joint statement, which you will advance with your vote today, is not only important, it is an indispensable step. First, it will allow you to exercise your legislative prerogatives by engaging in the trilogue negotiations with the Council to secure a common political agreement on the regulations implementing the deal. Second, it will strengthen our position in the engagement with the US to find solutions on outstanding issues such as steel and aluminum derivatives—a concern I know many of you share and rapporteur also mentioned it—and on securing additional tariff exemptions as agreed in the joint statement. Third, it paves the way for engaging in a wider positive agenda with the US, including on economic security, critical minerals, and trade facilitation measures. We need to show good faith on our part if we want to get good faith in return. At this stage, we have received reassurances from the US that they intend to honor the deal. Let me be clear on one thing: while we will continue our efforts for maintaining constructive relationships and avoid volatility, we will not turn a blind eye to any risks of our interests. The Commission will remain vigilant, ready to defend European interests whenever necessary. We are closely monitoring the US Section 301 investigations which may replace legal basis for US tariffs. We need to ensure that there are no risks in this respect, and if there are changes, they are only positive. Honorable members, today's vote is an important procedural step and a political signal that the EU stands by its word, that we act to protect our jobs, investments, and our economic prosperity. I look forward for hearing your views in the debate. Thank you. Ďakujem, pán komisár. A teraz za výbor pre rozpočet poprosím pani kolegyňu Nerudovú, nech sa páči. Dear Commissioner, dear colleagues. One day he wants to impose 40% tariffs, the next day he talks about occupying European territory. This is why we need a clear clause. We implement this deal only if the United States respect it. And if our territory is threatened again, the deal must be suspended immediately. So yes, I support the deal, but only if both sides, both sides respect it fully. Ďakujem. A ďalším bodom programu je správa, ktorú predkladajú poslankyňa Ridolfi a poslankyňa Bentele, Global Gateway: minulý vplyv a budúca orientácia. A na úvod debaty poprosím najprv pani poslankyňu Ridolfi, nech sa páči. Monsieur le Vice-Président, Madame la Commissaire Síkela, chère Hildegard, chers collègues. Je suis heureuse de co-présenter ce matin le premier rapport du Parlement européen sur l'initiative européenne Global Gateway. Global Gateway, c'est une initiative pharaonique d'investissement en infrastructures à travers le monde lancée en 2021 par l'Europe pour tenter de concurrencer le projet chinois notamment des nouvelles routes de la soie. C'est une initiative qui compte sur la mobilisation de financements publics des banques de développement mais aussi d'entreprises privées. Au-delà des annonces, des slogans, des montants colossaux qui sont affichés dans le cadre de cette initiative, on parle de 306 milliards d'euros, notre rapport note que Global Gateway reste encore trop opaque. Ses effets concrets restent méconnus. C'est un peu une boîte noire en quelque sorte. Notre rapport veut analyser avec honnêteté cet instrument en train de devenir structurant pour les relations extérieures de l'Union européenne au cœur de notre diplomatie économique. Dans un monde qui est marqué par la concurrence entre puissances, la recomposition géopolitique des dépendances, nous devons chercher à exister mais exister pourquoi ? Et au service de qui ? Ce qui frappe, c'est l'écart entre l'ambition affichée par l'initiative Global Gateway et les garanties réelles. Nous savons que Global Gateway finance des projets dans le numérique, l'énergie, les transports, la santé, l'éducation. Mais nous voulons qu'il constitue une alternative vertueuse respectueuse des droits et de la démocratie face aux investissements chinois, russes ou américains. La gouvernance de Global Gateway reste peu lisible avec des critères de sélection parfois inconnus et surtout des retombées pour les populations locales qui restent insuffisamment démontrées. Notre rapport insiste donc sur un point essentiel: nous ne pourrons pas être une alternative convaincante aux investissements chinois ou russes que si nous proposons des investissements et des partenariats fondés sur la transparence, la responsabilité et la relation d'égal à égal. Il en va de la qualité de nos relations avec nos partenaires et de la crédibilité de l'initiative Global Gateway. Nous demandons donc des données publiques sur les projets financés par Global Gateway, des études d'impact sérieuses, une consultation réelle des autorités locales, de la société civile et des mécanismes qui permettent de prévenir les violations des droits de l'homme et des dommages environnementaux. S'il n'y a pas cette traçabilité, il n'y aura pas de confiance et pas de succès. Nous devons veiller à l'exemplarité sociale de nos investissements car aucun projet d'infrastructure n'est neutre. Un corridor minier, un réseau numérique, un projet énergétique peuvent créer des emplois mais ils peuvent aussi exclure, déplacer, précariser tout un tas de populations. C'est pourquoi dans notre rapport nous défendons une approche plus exigeante pour la valeur ajoutée locale des projets pour les PME locales, la création d'emplois de qualité, la formation et les services publics. Global Gateway ne doit pas seulement servir les chaînes de valeur européennes, il doit aussi renforcer les économies locales, les capacités institutionnelles et la stabilité dans les pays partenaires mais aussi la santé, l'éducation, la recherche. J'aimerais enfin avoir un mot pour celles qui représentent la moitié de l'humanité, les femmes et les filles dans Global Gateway, elles doivent aussi compter car la diplomatie féministe est garante de stabilité et de prospérité. Global Gateway sera demain au cœur de la réalité économique, géopolitique de l'Union européenne, son succès déterminera l'avenir de nos relations avec le reste du monde. Et si nous voulons être à la hauteur des ambitions nous devrons prouver que nous ne finançons pas seulement des infrastructures mais aussi des droits, de l'égalité, des partenariats justes. C'est le sens du rapport que nous présentons aujourd'hui et je veux remercier l'ensemble des équipes qui ont travaillé avec nous, ma co-rapporteure Hildegard Bentele mais aussi toute notre équipe de shadow rapporteurs et bien sûr les secrétariats des commissions affaires étrangères et développement. Merci beaucoup et je me réjouis du débat que nous allons avoir. Merci. A teraz poprosím spravodajkyňu pani kolegyňu Bentele, nech sa páči. Lieber Herr Kommissar, liebe Chloe, liebe Kolleginnen und Kollegen, was könnten der südafrikanische Staatspräsident Ramaphosa, der kongolesische Staatspräsident Tshisekedi, der Staatspräsident von Kolumbien, der Premierminister von Armenien und der Premierminister von Grenada gemeinsam haben? Es gibt ein großes Interesse mit der Europäischen Union zusammenzuarbeiten, und zwar insbesondere und explizit auch mit europäischen Unternehmen, Ausbildungseinrichtungen, Investoren und Experten, Arbeitsplätze und Wertschöpfung vor Ort zu schaffen, Wirtschaftsentwicklung, Industrialisierung und Einbindung in internationale Lieferketten zu erreichen. Die Einrichtung eines Investment Hubs als One-Stop-Shop für Unternehmen und ein leider ziemlich katastrophales Meeting des Global Gateway Boards, in dem die Vertreter der Mitgliedstaaten und von Finanzierungsinstitutionen zusammenkommen. Hier liegt das Hauptproblem, das wir lösen müssen. Die Einbindung des Privatsektors und die Mobilisierung privaten Kapitals ist absolut zentral für die Initiative. Das heißt, dass wir stärker auf die Bedürfnisse des Privatsektors eingehen müssen. Das wiederum heißt: schnellere und transparentere Entscheidungen, klare Prioritäten, Planbarkeit, angepasste Finanzierungsinstrumente. Und dafür muss aus unserer Sicht insbesondere das Global Gateway Board deutlich operativer arbeiten. Häufigere Treffen, die Entscheidungsträger müssen an einen Tisch. Flagship-Projekte und unser Narrativ müssen klar herausgearbeitet werden, damit sie von unseren Partnerländern und auch von unseren Wettbewerbern als echte Leuchttürme wahrgenommen werden. Es gilt: raus aus dem Silo und dem Top-Down-Denken. Es gilt auf die Kohärenz unserer Politik als EU zu achten und unsere Interessen klar zu formulieren. Und das heißt in einer Zeit hoher geopolitischer Anspannung, dass wir auch in unseren Außenbeziehungen das Ziel der wirtschaftlichen Resilienz klar und deutlich in den Vordergrund stellen. Liebe Kolleginnen und Kollegen, liebe Bürgerinnen und Bürger, wirtschaftlich ist die EU ein absolut gefragter Partner. Wenn wir die Reformvorschläge zur Global Gateway Initiative, die wir Ihnen heute präsentieren, umsetzen, haben wir das Zeug wirklich zu liefern. Mehr Chancen für unsere europäischen Unternehmen global und ein deutlich stabileres Netz an Partnerschaften weltweit. Only sky is the limit. Vielen, vielen Dank. Grazie, grazie onorevole Bentele. Oura’s Tom Hale: What People Don’t Tell You About Being CEO

Sequoia Capital(133.0K) ·59:40 ·3.3K views · 2026-03-26
Tom Hale (CEO, Oura)
Oura CEO Tom Hale details the high-stress reality of the "kibble-to-champagne" ratio in leadership and provides a tactical blueprint for scaling through the "messy middle" (200–2,000 employees) by prioritizing work-to-headcount asymmetry and maintain...
TL;DR Oura CEO Tom Hale details the high-stress reality of the "kibble-to-champagne" ratio in leadership and provides a tactical blueprint for scaling through the "messy middle" (200–2,000 employees) by prioritizing work-to-headcount asymmetry and maintaining a non-hierarchical,...

TL;DR

Oura CEO Tom Hale details the high-stress reality of the "kibble-to-champagne" ratio in leadership and provides a tactical blueprint for scaling through the "messy middle" (200–2,000 employees) by prioritizing work-to-headcount asymmetry and maintaining a non-hierarchical, cross-continental culture.

Scaling Through the "Messy Middle" (200–2,000 Employees)

  • The Root of Office Politics: Hale argues that politics are caused by an Asymmetry of Work vs. People.
      • In a startup, work exceeds headcount, forcing focus.
      • In the "messy middle," if headcount exceeds work, employees become territorial over tasks and opportunities to prove value, causing politics to metastasize.
  • The "Ambitious" Middle Manager: To prevent bureaucracy (the "Dilbert" effect), Hale avoids hiring "professional" middle managers who seek to minimize complexity.
      • Internal Promotion: Favors promoting "true-blue believers" from within.
      • CEO Trackers: Specifically looks for middle managers whose ambition is to eventually become a CEO, as they are the most effective "prophylactic against bureaucracy."
  • Radical Visibility: Hale maintains culture by "lurking" in Slack channels at all levels.
      • Direct Interaction: By commenting on low-level work or challenging ideas directly in public channels, he fosters a culture where ideas move fluidly regardless of hierarchy.

The Controversial Pivot to Subscription

  • Strategic Necessity: Oura shifted to a $6/month subscription to decouple value from the hardware replacement cycle.
      • Disincentivizing Obsolescence: Traditional hardware models discourage software updates for old devices to force new purchases; subscription models mandate "earning your stripes" every month through continuous software value.
  • The "Only Way Out is Through": During the initial rollout, Oura faced significant backlash on Reddit and social media.
      • Sticking to the Vision: Hale briefly considered a rollback during a "painful" moment in the Salesforce Tower garage but concluded that retreating would permanently damage the brand's credibility.
      • V-to-P Ratio: Success is maintained by keeping the Value-to-Price ratio above 1.5x or 2x. If the perceived value of health insights (e.g., illness prediction or pregnancy tracking) far outweighs $6, retention remains high.
  • Complexity Kills: Hale resists multi-tiered pricing. He views the "gap" between what a customer would pay and what they actually pay as brand goodwill that pays dividends in word-of-mouth marketing.

Global Culture: Finland vs. The United States

  • Managing Different Norms: Oura balances a Finnish "socialist/non-hierarchical" culture with an American "capitalist/performance" culture.
      • The Monoculture Trap: Hale rejects the idea of a single corporate monoculture, preferring that the two distinct cultures "coexist and stimulate each other."
  • Building Social Capital: To bridge the gap in a remote/hybrid world, Oura funds intense, 2–3 day in-person team meetups.
      • Banking Trust: Hale believes humans are optimized for in-person connection; high-intensity meetups "bank" social capital that teams can draw upon when working remotely later.

Growth Strategy: High-End Partnerships and Retail

  • The Gucci Case Study: A $999 limited-edition ring validated Oura as a fashion object rather than just a utility.
      • Pricing Confidence: Gucci CEO Marco Bizzarri pushed the $999 price point, insisting that Oura’s tech combined with Gucci’s design created an "object of desire" that defied standard utility pricing.
      • Retail Insight: The Gucci partnership served as a "first channel partner," proving that customers wanted to see the product in person. This paved the way for Oura’s expansion into Best Buy, Target, and Costco.
  • Competitive Moat vs. Apple: Hale is not threatened by the Apple Watch or a potential "Apple Ring."
      • Complementarity: Two-thirds of Oura users wear a second wearable (usually an Apple Watch). Oura dominates the "night/recovery" niche while the watch handles "day/utility."
      • Signal Strength: Measuring from the finger provides a signal 50–100x stronger than the wrist, offering a technical advantage in data quality for AI inferences.

Notable Quotes

"The ratio of kibble to champagne favors the kibble... Participating in failure? You get to own that failure. Because in many cases, it was either your decision, your direction, or the system that you set up." — Tom Hale
"Above 2,000 [employees], you have this much work and you have this many people, which means they’re fighting for the best work. And that’s politics." — Tom Hale
"You gotta be comfortable being uncomfortable. That is the nature of [being CEO]. You gotta let that discomfort sit... because that’s actually where the magic happens." — Tom Hale
"Complexity kills. You gotta make it simple so that they can do the equation in their head: 'Am I getting enough value for this $6?'" — Tom Hale
Just by paying attention to the work that was happening at all levels of the company, from the lowest to the highest, by paying attention and being like, “Hey, that’s a really good idea,” or “Have you considered this?” It made a culture of: it’s okay to have ideas and have them move up and down. And, by the way, people would challenge me. And in challenging me, then they felt confident that like we were going to get to the best idea because it wasn’t just my idea. Creating this sort of non-hierarchical culture that’s not bound by time or geography or role? Really powerful. Okay, this episode is with Tom Hale, an old friend of mine who’s the CEO of Oura, of Oura Ring fame. When I was in my early fifties, I had a very bad snowmobile accident. I drove a snowmobile off a cliff, and you can’t see my body, but it’s absolutely chock full of metal. And I thought I was going to die that night. I was lying at the bottom of the cliff and, I’ve been CEO of HubSpot for 15 years, like, I’m done. I’m tired, I’m done, I’m burnt out. He went the other way. You know, he had been kind of a corporate exec at some really interesting companies his whole career. In his early fifties, he’s like, “I want my shot.” And so we talked about that, what he didn’t expect from the job: the good, the bad, and the downright ugly of being a CEO. He’s pretty thoughtful about that. What really is interesting to me about Tom is he spent his entire life in companies between 200 and 2,000 employees. That is the messy middle. That’s where things slow down. That’s where bureaucracy happens. That’s where middle managers happen. That’s where you really slow the gears of progress down. And we talk a bunch about what it takes to keep that engine revving through that and how not to get caught in a quagmire. We also talk, he’s got a team in Finland. This company was started in Finland, and he’s got a team here in the US. And, man, are they different. The Americans are obviously very capitalist creatures and the Finnish are much more on the socialist side. And we talk about how to manage those two. And a lot of you are in the US now and thinking about going international. I think there’s a lot of lessons here for you. So, lots of good stuff. I’m going to come back at the end with my take on what he had to say. Thanks for being here. Hey, thanks for having me, Brian. I have a little story for you. Okay. Four years ago, I was snowmobiling in Woodstock, Vermont. As one does. Yes. And with my son. And the snowmobile went off a cliff. Oh my god. It hit the bottom. Snowmobile’s in a million pieces, my son and I were in a million pieces. And at the bottom of that, and no one knew where we were, and it was in the afternoon. I was pretty sure we were both going to die that night. And while I was sitting there at the bottom of the cliff, I said, “I need to make some big changes in my life.” No more snowmobiles. Yes, that too. But the biggest change was: I don’t want to be a CEO anymore. Why did you want to be a CEO after all these years? Wow, that’s a good question. I think the obvious answer, and maybe the really the true answer, is it was a bucket list item I had to check off my list. Yes. And the thing is, actually, and maybe this is TMI, but I also wanted to prove to myself that I had it, to do it. Is it everything you had hoped? And more. Really? Much, much harder than I thought. And I’m sure you can appreciate that, and any CEO in the world will appreciate that. Much harder, much harder than I thought. What’s harder than you thought? It’s not the work that’s harder, it’s the responsibility and the stress. It’s the waking up at 3 AM and being like, “Oh my god, is this going to work? And what is it going to take to make it work?” That, I think, it’s pressure, it’s stress, it’s responsibility. It’s all the people that you have, you know, they’ve put their faith in you, whether it’s your board or your employees or your customers, and you have to carry that and, you know what they say, the buck stops here. Absolutely true. I felt that, too. I was pretty calm in the first four or five years of HubSpot, but I was kind of stricken with stress and started having, I’d never had them before, panic attacks like five years in. And it was that. It was like, “Oh man, we have 100 employees and they’re really counting on me.” 100%. What do people misunderstand about CEOing? Well, I think they think it’s a lot more fun than it is. Okay, so is it not fun? No, I mean, I don’t think actually it’s not fun. I think there is fun. I just think that the sort of ratio of kibble, you know, to champagne favors the kibble than the champagne. And I think that part of it is the responsibility which we just talked about. But part of it is also you kind of, the things about work that I really enjoy, like building something in a fine grain of detail and making it beautiful and owning that and feeling pride in that. And I still get that but just not as much. Okay, so the first thing is you keep as few layers between the top and the bottom, meaning, you know, you don’t have senior directors and directors and VPs and senior VPs. Like you just try and kind of resist that for as long as possible. I think this is famously a flat organization if you can do that. So I think that’s – that’s one way to do it. I think the second one is when you think about middle management, I often favor promoting people internally in the company at to middle management as opposed to hiring them. Now I don’t always do that, but the one of the reasons you do that is because you’re able to identify both the kinds of people who might be good at it, but also you’re able to identify: are they true-blue believers and do they have the passion and the mission? Because the worst thing you can have is somebody who’s in middle management who has no ambition. Because that’s actually the Dilbert. The Dilbert is like, “I don’t want my life to be complicated, I want my work life to be as as manageable as possible and I’m going to manage everything to be managed,” and that’s the definition of an empty suit, right? What you hire is an ambitious middle manager whose ambition is to be a CEO and know that at some point they’re going to leave because they’re going to have to. But that ambitious middle manager is one of the most effective prophylactics against sort of the propagation of bureaucracy. I think the other thing that you do is you say, “Listen, we really are going to empower you and we’re going to hold you accountable,” and then you make that true. You can’t hire a dog and then bark for them. You have to hire somebody and say like, “You have got to do this and I’m going to hold you accountable for it. Now I might have some advice for you and I might have some guidance, and like, but you got to do it.” I think it comes back sort of a variation on ambitious middle management, but it’s basically, “We’re going to give you the keys to the car. So drive fast, but don’t crash.” And I think the the last piece of it is you create a company culture that reinforces that that’s like risk-taking, you know, we’re going to we’re going to let you take some risk and but we’re going to support you through that and we’re going to give you the the best advice we can. It goes back to this asymmetry thing where if you have enough time and energy to talk to people in middle management, you can one root out the people who are the Dilberts because you’re talking to them. You’ve been through some dramatic moments in your career. and I want to get to that, but one of those dramatic moments was you made a business model shift. Historically you just bought the ring and that was it. Typical hardware model. You introduced a six-dollar-a-month subscription service. Can you – and it was controversial, customers were not happy – just take us behind the scenes of that decision. Was that kind of percolating in the org in your head? Was that the first thing you did when you came in as a CEO? Was it contentious within the leadership team? it was the strategy. And the strategy was, in order to be the most competitive hardware company in the world, you needed to be a really great software company. And to be a really great software company, you needed a business model and a way to deliver periodic software value. The problem with the hardware model is that you ship your hardware and it’s fixed, and that’s it. And you might ship a, you know, a little bit of a software update or a firmware update or something, but like really, you’re not really changing the value proposition for that product with new capabilities. And the reason why is because when you ship the next version of the hardware, you want all that value to go in that next version of the hardware so someone moves from their iPhone 13 to their iPhone 14. So you’re disincentivized actually to provide value to your customer over time. The power of a subscription model is that you got to kind of earn your stripes with your customers every month. Yeah. Every month. Now by the way, six dollars a month for Oura is kind of a low bar. Whatever, two cups of coffee. And if we’re delivering information that helps you with your health, the value of that is so priceless. Like the two times, you know, that that we help you get to bed early or we predict that you’re going to be sick or we tell you when to, you know, procreate in order to have a baby, like, the value of that is so high that the the ratio of V to P, value to price, is measured in multiples. Was it contentious inside the org? Okay, so I’m getting to that, right? But the thing is I’m saying it was our strategy. Yeah. You brought that in as your strategy, from what I understand. well, no, actually, it had already – it was already there. And I think there were things that I did maybe to sort of tune it a little bit, but – and I don’t even want to say I, I’d say the team did this. But the – was it contentious? It was contentious. And partially it was contentious because going against the grain of expectation. Hardware should be a single price, was contentious. And this idea that like – I don’t know, you probably have some subscriptions that you would you would be really sad if you stopped paying for them, right? Like Netflix and Spotify or something like that. You probably use those things and if you if you were forced to give them away – at HubSpot! – and if you were forced to give them away you’d be really sad. Yes. Right. So the idea is like, well, if you can do that, then you probably have a right to charge for subscription. Now that being said, there are lots of subscriptions that you probably pay for that you don’t get any value from. And you continue to pay because of either inertia or whatever. And those things, we used to call them in the back of the days we called them zombie subscriptions and the customer who used them were sleeping bears. Yeah. And you know what you don’t do with sleeping bears? Poke them. Yeah, you don’t poke them. And so as a result what you want to do is avoid the situation where you got a bunch of customers who are not getting value but you’re still collecting price. In which case the value is low and the P is high. You want to avoid that. So I think we felt that that was the strategy and that was going to be okay. So that controversy part actually was relatively easy to kind of navigate because we said, listen, one, there’s a reason that we’re doing this. We’re creating a business model that will allow us to invest in software value and deliver feature value and analysis value on your health over a periods of time. And like the product will get better every month and like SaaS, you’re getting a new release every time. And by the way, turns out that worked. We have some of the like literally the best retention I have ever seen in my 30 years of working in subscription business at Oura. And that’s because the value that we deliver is constantly a multiple of the P, the price that we take. So that was that was that part. The second part of it was: “But what about all our customers who bought a product? How can we introduce a subscription for them?” And that was easy. That was: give them a pathway to get to effectively what they had before. And so in the in our version of it, if you were a Gen 2 customer, if you wanted to get a Gen 3 and you paid to get a Gen 3 – and we gave upgrade discounts, which is crazy, who gives upgrade discounts on hardware? – if you if you did, you would be a lifetime subscriber. And it was to make that transition in business model as smooth as possible. And so in doing that, actually tremendous value because if you’re a lifetime subscriber for the, you know, committing to just the next version, you were going to get free software for the rest of your, you know, time that you wore an Oura Ring. Great deal, in fact, some of our most loyal highest retaining customers are those customers because they have no disincentive to churn. So that was the second thing. And then the third thing, and this was a little bit like the genius of it. Can you buy a HubSpot in a like a perpetual license model? No. No. Why not? Because that’s how it’s sold. Same thing. I think people have an expectation about subscriptions that it’s the freemium business model. I give you 50 percent of the value for free because I want you to try the application and then if you pay you’ll get the other 50 percent. And we did something very different. We said, “It is a subscription product. And if you pay, the product will be as functional as it is and if you don’t pay it’ll collapse down to be very minimally functional.” It’s not it won’t work, but it’s not 50 percent of the value. And so as a result, interestingly, when we rolled out the subscriptions, what we saw was that some people were like, “Oh, I’m not going to pay!” A lot of people. Well, actually, less than you would think. Oh! I remember hearing – It was a lot of agitation, it was a lot of agitation. But like the reality was – For $6/month, there was a lot of noise. It was a lot of noise. And I think, you know, you make a bargain with your customer, it’s hard to change that, no question about that. But what would happen is like someone would see the product and then they’d stop paying for whatever reason. Sometimes it’s just like their credit card died and we didn’t update it, whatever. And it would collapse down to sort of its non-subscription kind of experience. And then they’d be like, 24 hours later they would renew. Because they would see that the value that they were getting was absolutely worth that price. And I think that that is the key. If you can keep that value over price ratio north of 1.5 or 2, then you know you’re doing it right and your customers are going to reward you with retention and loyalty. Okay. The first surprise is it’s actually way more stressful than you thought when you started it. And for me, where it got really stressful wasn’t so much like 10 employees. It was when we hit 100 employees and I just started thinking, “Man, all those employees are counting on me. Their spouse and their kids are counting on me. A lot of times their parents are counting on me.” And so my stress level really cranked around 100. I started getting help for it; I got on an SSRI. I was having panic attacks and kind of calmed myself down. But the ironic thing is the bigger the organization gets, kind of the more pressure’s on you. The other thing that seems to surprise most first-time CEOs is really you’re constantly selling. You’re selling to accounts, customers. You’re selling to potential employees, you’re selling to investors, you’re selling to partners. You’re just always selling. And that surprises particularly technical founders. Your VCs aren’t going to save you, your co-founder’s not going to save you. The responsibility is much, much bigger than being an exec in another startup. Like Parker, from his first startup blowing up to his big drama with Deel. Almost all of them have really traumatic crises, and that can kill a company quite easily unless you’re quite thick-skinned. The other thing that kind of happened to me is the larger the company got, I noticed that the company and I—like my brand—kind of merged with the company. And everything I said and everything I did, all the employees, the market, everyone’s watching everything very carefully. And they may not have pretended they were listening that carefully, but if I said something in the hallway, like, “Wouldn’t it be great if we had this feature?” five minutes later someone was coding it. So you really can’t think out loud that much as a CEO. Those are some of the surprises of a first-time CEO. He talked about a couple of those; a bunch of the other ones I’ve picked up from the CEOs I work with. Hopefully you’re avoiding some of them. Tom is an expert in going from 200 to 2,000 and not losing your mind on the way. And typically what happens from 200 to 2,000 is things really start to slow down and lots of companies kind of die in there. A couple things to avoid that quagmire. One is there’s an old expression: hire slow and fire fast. It’s very old and it is very true. Now it’s easier said than done. Lots of founders tell me, “The first time I thought I should have fired somebody, I should have fired them because I always end up firing them.” I say that same thing, but I never really did it. I was always more patient than I should have been, but I’m also a homo sapien and it was hard for me to do that. The second thing I would say is you hit 200 employees and maybe get some VPs and managers—that’s when the director layer shows up. And in particular, when you hire that director layer from the outside, they’re kind of a professional middle manager. So push that director layer off as long as you can. Keep the span of control—maybe it’s not Jensen Huang’s 60, but keep it wide and avoid that middle layer. The thing that really helped HubSpot is we talked about EV: enterprise value, versus TV: your team’s value, versus MV: your own value. And the thing that happens inside of companies is that as they get bigger, people don’t solve for the enterprise as much; they solve for the team. Like when they solve for their team, they kind of sub-optimize for the team next to them. So that TV is where a lot of execs in a lot of companies fall down. So I recommend that. I wrote on whiteboards all over the place: EV > TV > MV. And I kind of pounded that into the company. Anytime someone in a meeting was talking very TV, I would push on the EV. I don’t think you should have all "been there, done that" folks. I think you should have 50 percent "been there, done that," and 50 percent homegrown. That institutional knowledge for the homegrown is super useful. People underestimate the value of homegrown and how people can grow and learn. So I like that ratio. And the last thing I would just say is Elon—this is a big Elon thing. I was at the Sequoia’s Basecamp event must have been 10 years ago where he did this vector alignment thing. And he described all his employees as vectors, and there were different sizes; some people were more powerful than others, but they were kind of all pointed in different directions. And he said the key for him is no matter how big or small the employee is, how do you get them all pointed in the same direction to get stuff done? Vector alignment and a little tiny bit of lightweight planning can help you a lot with that. Those are some of my tricks to not fall into the 200 to 2,000 trap. Okay, Tom had some interesting thoughts on pricing; I do too. You know, when Moses came down from the mountain, it’s well known they had ten commandments: five on one tablet, five on the other. It’s not as well known that there was a third tablet with an 11th and a 12th commandment. The 11th commandment was: over time, your product’s pricing will go up. The 12th was: over time, your product’s pricing will get more complicated. Now your sales organization loves that, your products don’t. What I liked about what Tom did, he shifted his business model to subscription, but he kept it relatively cheap. It’s six bucks a month. And you know, he didn’t overcomplicate it. It was quite simple. And I think a move for founders is like, if the supply and demand curve looks like this, people very quickly rush to almost perfectly match that supply and demand curve: the willingness to pay, with the pricing model. And that encourages the pricing model to get more complicated and more expensive. I think that’s fine when you’re 50 years old, but in startup mode I think you want a gap between those two things. You want a less adversarial relationship with your customers. You want to be delivering maximum value so you get word of mouth. And that gap is goodwill, and that goodwill pays itself back in spades. So don’t maximize that supply and demand curve would be my advice. Okay, those are my thoughts on Tom. Some really good tips in there, I thought. I hope you enjoyed it, and I’ll see you on the next episode of Long Strange Trip.

Terafab: Elon’s Plan To Dominate Semiconductors | The Brainstorm EP 124

ARK Invest(634.0K) ·32:12 ·7.2K views · 2026-03-26
Brett Winton (Chief Futurist, ARK Invest) Nick Grous (Associate Portfolio Manager, ARK Invest) Sam Korus (Director of Research, ARK Invest)
Elon Musk’s "TeraFab" project is a proposed massive-scale, vertically integrated semiconductor facility designed to eliminate the supply-chain "choke point" for AI and space exploration, aiming to provide the "terawatts of chips" necessary for a futu...
TL;DR Elon Musk’s "TeraFab" project is a proposed massive-scale, vertically integrated semiconductor facility designed to eliminate the supply-chain "choke point" for AI and space exploration, aiming to provide the "terawatts of chips" necessary for a future of infinite...

TL;DR

Elon Musk’s "TeraFab" project is a proposed massive-scale, vertically integrated semiconductor facility designed to eliminate the supply-chain "choke point" for AI and space exploration, aiming to provide the "terawatts of chips" necessary for a future of infinite intelligence and orbital infrastructure.

The TeraFab Vision and Scale

  • Vertical Integration: The TeraFab concept involves bringing every stage of the semiconductor supply chain—chip design, memory production, advanced packaging, and testing—under a single roof.
      • The proposed facility is described as being three times the size of Central Park.
      • Musk intends for designers to sit directly next to manufacturing equipment to allow for instant testing and rapid iteration.
  • Capital and Power Requirements: The reported initial "kick-starter" capital for the project is $20–$25 billion.
      • Power Demand: The facility is estimated to require 10 gigawatts of power. For context, building 10GW of nuclear power capacity alone would cost at least $10 billion.
  • The "Third Ingredient": Sam Korus identifies semiconductors as the final missing piece for the future of civilization, alongside solar panels and launch capacity (Starship).

Market Dynamics: "Risk-On" vs. Boom-Bust Cycles

  • Industry Conservatism: Traditional players like TSMC are often hesitant to overbuild capacity due to the semiconductor industry's historical "boom-bust" nature, which can kill margins if demand dips.
  • Infinite Demand for Intelligence: Musk is operating on a "risk-on" wager that there is near-infinite demand for AI compute. He is willing to utilize his own balance sheet (Tesla, SpaceX, xAI) to build capacity that the rest of the industry deems too risky.
  • Competitive Pressure: Brett Winton suggests that TeraFab acts as a "negotiating ploy." If Musk begins to make progress, it will force traditional suppliers to increase their own CapEx to ensure MegaCap Tech companies are not left at a competitive disadvantage against the Musk conglomerate.

Orbital Infrastructure and the Lunar "Mass Driver"

  • Data Centers in Space: A significant portion of the chips produced by TeraFab (roughly 80%) are intended for orbital infrastructure rather than terrestrial robots.
      • Space-based data centers avoid the increasing difficulty of building and powering massive facilities on Earth.
  • The Lunar Mass Driver: Musk envisions building a "mass driver" (effectively a railgun) on the moon to launch satellites into space.
      • Because the moon’s gravity is a fraction of Earth's, this would be a significantly cheaper method for servicing Earth-orbit constellations.
  • The Optimus Connection: Construction on the moon would likely be handled by Optimus humanoid robots working alongside humans. The timeline for these projects is heavily dependent on the reusability of Starship and the maturation of Optimus.

"Digital Optimus" and SaaS Disruption

  • Disrupting Software as a Service (SaaS): The team discussed "Digital Optimus"—AI agents that can interface with any digital application exactly like a human user.
  • Eliminating Gatekeepers: These agents could programmatically access and extract data from SaaS platforms that currently lack open APIs or try to "lock in" user data.
      • This is viewed as "hugely corrosive" to existing SaaS companies whose business models rely on controlling the user interface and data visualization.
"Elon always looks forward a few years at the choke point that he anticipates impinging upon his strategic plans... and access to chips is his anticipated choke point." — Brett Winton
"There's three ingredients for the future of civilization: solar panels, launch capacity, and semiconductors. The first two are taken care of... this third ingredient is the missing ingredient and that is where TeraFab comes in." — Sam Korus
"His wager is: I want as much compute as possible because I think that math continues to work no matter how many gigawatts I throw at the problem." — Brett Winton
Hello everyone and welcome back to another episode of The Brainstorm. This week we're talking TeraFab. What if I told you the entire global semiconductor industry isn't building fast enough? At least according to Elon Musk. Today we're talking about what was announced, what's credible, and what's just a pure moonshot. Sam, Brett, help break it down for us. Okay, I'll start at the very high, highest level there is. There's three ingredients for the future of civilization: solar panels, launch capacity, and semiconductors. The first two are taken care of. SpaceX is on its way with Starship to roughly a hundred dollars per kilogram to low earth orbit, potentially below that launch capability. Trump, Xi to Meet in Beijing on May 14–15 | The China Show 3/26/2026

Bloomberg Television(3.1M) ·92:58 ·19.5K views · 2026-03-26
Donald Trump (President of the United States) Xi Jinping (President of China)
President Trump and Xi Jinping are scheduled to meet in Beijing on May 14–15, 2026, as the U.S.
TL;DR President Trump and Xi Jinping are scheduled to meet in Beijing on May 14–15, 2026, as the U.S. insists the Iran war is entering its final stages despite public rejection of peace terms by Tehran. Markets are currently navigating a massive $1.1 trillion Chinese earnings...

TL;DR

President Trump and Xi Jinping are scheduled to meet in Beijing on May 14–15, 2026, as the U.S. insists the Iran war is entering its final stages despite public rejection of peace terms by Tehran. Markets are currently navigating a massive $1.1 trillion Chinese earnings wave and a global energy crunch triggered by the continued closure of the Strait of Hormuz.

Geopolitics: The Trump-Xi Summit and Iran Conflict

  • Beijing Summit Scheduled: The White House confirmed President Trump will meet Xi Jinping in Beijing on May 14–15. Analysts suggest a cessation of Middle East hostilities is likely a prerequisite for the summit to ensure diplomatic "deliverables" are not overshadowed by the war.
  • Divergent Iran Narratives:
      • U.S. Stance: President Trump claims Iran is "negotiating" and wants a deal "badly," suggesting the conflict is in its final stages.
      • Reality Gap: Bloomberg Politics Editor Laura Davison reports the two sides remain at "opposite ends of the spectrum," with Iran publicly rejecting U.S. outreach and demanding continued authority over the Strait of Hormuz.
  • Military Posturing: While discussing peace, the U.S. continues to move thousands of troops to the region—a logistical shift that typically signals an intent to deploy rather than rotate.

Energy and Shipping: Navigating the Global Fuel Crunch

  • Strait of Hormuz Disruption: The waterway remains largely closed to shipping. Raymond Peter (Bernhard Schulte Shipmanagement) reports that many managers are refusing to transit, prioritizing crew safety over premiums as insurance costs soar.
  • National Oil Reserves: Japan has begun releasing 8.5 million kiloliters of national oil reserves (approx. $3.4 billion) to stabilize prices. Simultaneously, South Korea and the Philippines have urged citizens to reduce power use amid energy emergencies.
  • China’s Relative Stability: Denis Depoux (Roland Berger) notes China is proving more resilient than India or Southeast Asia during this shock due to its aggressive electrification of vehicle fleets and "energy buffer" investments.

Chinese Markets: Earnings Volatility and Regulatory Shifts

  • End of the "Price Wars": Chinese state media issued a stern directive to food delivery platforms (Meituan, Alibaba, JD.com) to end "frenzied" subsidy wars. Regulators estimate $11 billion to $14.5 billion was spent on subsidies last year, contributing to a "deflationary spiral."
  • Corporate Winners and Losers:
      • Pop Mart: Shares plummeted nearly 30% over two days following earnings that revealed a dangerous over-reliance on the "Labubu" toy line (40% of sales) and a sharp deceleration in growth for other IPs.
      • Kuaishou: Stock fell 13% as investors reacted poorly to heavy AI-related capital expenditures, despite the company meeting revenue estimates.
      • Baozun (Gap China): CEO Vincent Qiu reported Gap China’s first break-even quarter since acquisition, attributing success to a "Yun-Yeung" localization strategy (mixing U.S. brand assets with local supply chains).

Infrastructure and Investment: The Shift to Security

  • Energy Security over ESG: Michael Dorrell (Stonepeak) noted that the global energy crisis has shifted the investment pendulum back toward traditional energy infrastructure (gas/oil), which is now prioritized alongside ESG goals.
  • Data Center Acceleration: Despite "AI scare" headlines, Stonepeak reports an acceleration in demand, having sold five years' worth of data center inventory in just the last six months.
  • Hong Kong’s Safe Haven Status: Financial Secretary Paul Chan highlighted over $90 billion in net capital inflows last year, emphasizing a pipeline of over 400 mainland companies currently queuing for IPOs in Hong Kong.

Notable Quotes

"We settled eight wars people sort of forget and we're winning another one, I tell you, we're winning so big. Nobody's ever seen anything like we're doing in the Middle East with Iran. And they are negotiating, by the way, and they want to make a deal so badly."

— Donald Trump, President of the United States

"It is essential that we implement the vision of global governance featuring extensive consultation... [and] reject clash of civilizations and ideological confrontation... China will further expand high-standard opening up."

— Zhao Leji, Chairman of the Standing Committee of the National People's Congress

"It’s time to end the food delivery price war... the frenzied food delivery war must be put out. [Subsidies] are hurting the restaurant sector and continuing this deflationary spiral."

— Excerpts from Chinese State Media column

"We call this 'Yun-Yeung'—like the Hong Kong drink mixing tea and coffee. We take the coffee (U.S. brand assets) and mix it with the tea (local customer voice and supply chain). You have to be faster and more localized to meet the market."

— Vincent Qiu, Founder and CEO of Baozun

"I think we would be able to better assess the situation in April or May when things are more settled."
